It can be said that this is the difference between the command of Hashem and the speech of Moshe Rabbeinu to Israel: Due to the nature of the Kohanim’s souls, it would be appropriate for them to be zealous in everything pertaining to the matters of Kehuna, and therefore, regarding the command of Hashem, there is no need to warn the Beis Din regarding the Kohanim. But since we have been commanded to "make a fence for the Torah," Moshe Rabbeinu added a "fence" on his own initiative. Even though when the Kohanim are engaged in the *avodah* of the Kehuna we say they are zealous because the awe of the holy service is upon them, when they are not engaged in their *avodah*, it is possible that there could be a weakness in their zeal, and therefore there is a need to urge them regarding this.

E. This can be explained in terms of *mussar* and *avodah*:

The essence of the Kohanim is—in the words of the Rambam—"to be set apart to stand before Hashem to serve Him and minister to Him... and to cast off from his neck the yoke of the many calculations, etc. He is sanctified as holy of holies, and Hashem will be his portion and his inheritance forever and ever, etc." From this it is understood that one who stands at the level of Kehuna according to its essence feels in every place and at every time that he is standing "before Hashem," and consequently, there is no need to urge him. Only a Kohen who is not at this level needs zeal in his Kehuna matters when he is not engaged in the *avodah* of the Beis Hamikdash.

We can add an explanation according to the inner dimension of these matters:

It is explained in the Tanya that "the Ein Sof, blessed be He, is clothed in the faculty of *Chochma* in the soul of man, whoever he may be among Israel." In this faculty, even though the concept of *galus* does not apply to it—that the *nefesh habehamis* which is from the *kelipah* could rule over it, etc.—nevertheless, the concept of "sleep" is possible for it: "It is in a state of sleep in the wicked and does not perform its function."

However, there is a deeper faculty in the soul of man to which even the definition of "sleep" does not apply, and that is the essence of the soul (as is explained in the books of Kabbalah that "there is a very small spark which is a level of *Elokus*, etc., and this spark is clothed in the power of a created spark, etc., which is called *yechidah*"). Regarding this it is said, "I am asleep, but my heart is awake"—the essence and inwardness of the heart, which is always awake in every single Yisroel, and to which even sleep does not apply.

These are the two matters in every Yisroel: Due to the spark of the Creator clothed in his *yechidah*, there is no need for any urging, for it is always in perfection in every place and at every time,

## 66

in the language of the verse, "Who dwells with them in the midst of their *tumah*." Even in the Diaspora and in the time of *galus*, there is no need to warn the Kohen regarding separation from *tumah*.

And only due to the faculty of *Chochma* in the soul—where the concept of sleep is applicable as mentioned above—is there a need to warn the Beis Din regarding the Kohanim.

F. But nevertheless, even in this faculty, it is only "sleep" alone and not *galus*, Heaven forbid. Therefore, the arousal of the *Chochma* in the soul is not connected with an action by way of a complete renewal (nullification of evil, etc.), but rather it is a matter of waking from sleep alone.

Based on this, we can explain the precision of the language of the Rebbe, our master and teacher, in one of his *sichos*:

"It is the time when we are living at the head of the mountain. In truth, one sees that Mashiach is already near; he is behind the wall, and one who has a good sense of hearing and a sense of sight hears his voice and sees... Before the light of the day, the sleep intensifies... One must overcome and not sleep through the time... And then one will also be a vessel to receive the light of the day. This is something every Yisroel must know, and also that when he encounters a Yisroel, he must say to him: ‘Listen, brother, do not sleep through the light of the day,’" end of his holy words.

From this, it is understood that there is no need to create within him the desire to overcome and perform actions to urge the morning light of the *Geulah*, but rather one only needs to awaken him from his sleep, and consequently, every Yisroel will certainly do his part to bring the *Geulah*, immediately and truly.

(From the *sichos* of Shabbos Parshas Emor, 5725)

## 67

### Sicha 2

A. At the end of our Parsha it is stated: "And the son of an Israelite woman went out, and he was the son of an Egyptian man... and the son of the Israelite woman pronounced the Name and cursed, and they brought him to Moshe; and his mother’s name was Shelomis the daughter of Dibri, of the tribe of Dan." Our Sages of blessed memory said that this verse comes to teach us "that [the Jewish people] were not suspected of illicit relations"—"to announce the praise of the Jewish people, that there was not among them [any such case] except for this one, and the Torah publicized and detailed it; and regarding them it is explicitly stated in the Kabbalah: 'A locked garden is my sister, my bride, a locked spring, a sealed fountain.'" This is also brought in Rashi: "And his mother’s name was Shelomis the daughter of Dibri—the praise of the Jewish people, that the Torah publicized this one to say that she alone was [the exception]."

Now, even according to this interpretation, the failure of Shelomis the daughter of Dibri was not intentional, as the Ra'am proves here from the words of Rashi in Parshas Shemos: "From what is written... regarding the striking of the Hebrew man, that the husband of Shelomis the daughter of Dibri was there, and [the Egyptian] set his eyes upon her. At night, he set him up and sent him out of his house, and he [the Egyptian] returned and entered her house and had relations with his wife while she thought it was her husband. The man returned to his house and sensed the matter, and when he saw that the Egyptian sensed the matter, he would strike and beat him all day—this implies that she was deceived and it was not [an act of] will."

Based on this, we can understand the magnitude of the praise of the Jewish people learned from here: that the fact that the other women of Israel did not fail in this regard means that there was not even a case of being coerced or deceived.

Therefore, the Torah informs us, "And his mother’s name was Shelomis the daughter of Dibri"—even though it is not the way of the Torah to publicize and announce the disgrace of the Jewish people, and certainly in this case where we are speaking of an error—the goal is not to publicize the disgrace of an Israelite woman, Heaven forbid, but rather the opposite: to publicize the praise of the Jewish people, that failure in illicit relations was entirely ruled out for them (not even by accident or coercion).

## 62

Regarding Rashi’s continuation in his commentary, "Shelomis—she was a chatterbox, 'Peace upon you, peace upon you, peace upon all of you,' she would chatter with words, asking after everyone’s welfare; 'daughter of Dibri'—she was a talker, she would speak with every person," the commentators have already written that his intent is to explain that even she herself was not publicized for disgrace in this verse, for this is not her proper name, but rather a name based on her actions (and thus, who this woman is was not publicized).

Yet, one still needs to understand the fundamental issue of the Torah recounting the disgrace of an Israelite woman, whoever she may be (even if through this the praise of the Jewish people is publicized), when even "regarding the disgrace of a non-kosher animal, the Torah did not speak."

B. According to the simple meaning, it can be said, by way of introduction to what is written in the commentators, that the reason the Torah elaborates on the details of the lineage of the blasphemer is to explain how it is possible that a man from the children of Israel would come to curse the Name, Heaven forbid. In truth, such a thing is impossible for a Jewish man, except that because his father was an Egyptian man and his mother was coerced because she was a chatterbox, since he was not of pure seed, he descended to such a level that "he pronounced the Name and cursed."

Based on this, it can be said that just as the very story of the blasphemer’s act is not a matter of the Torah telling and publicizing the disgrace of the Jewish people—because the intent of the verse is not to tell of the disgrace but to inform us of the law and the significant precedent, the law of the blasphemer, his death penalty, and the details of the laws connected to him (as well as the details of the laws stated in connection and relation to this law, "And to the children of Israel you shall speak, saying")—so too is it regarding the lineage and conduct of his mother. It is in order to warn most strongly about the conduct and modesty of a daughter of Israel, not to be a chatterbox (even in the manner of asking after everyone’s welfare) and not to be a talker, since from such conduct it is possible that it will lead not only to her own corruption, but that a son will be born who will curse, Heaven forbid.

And more than this: the fact that as a result of this act a son was born who cursed is a wonder, for she did not fail in this intentionally, but was deceived, and the fact that she was "asking after everyone’s welfare" was not a matter of immodesty, but a matter of conduct with a pleasant countenance toward everyone, and nevertheless, there emerged from this a son such as this who cursed, Heaven forbid—and this is a special sign to provide the greatest possible warning to everyone regarding the magnitude of the caution required in modesty, etc.

## 63

According to this, there is room to say even more: that in the fact that the Torah publicized her, there is also an aspect of her praise, for through her, the Jewish people learned the magnitude of the caution required in the matter of modesty.

[Similar to what is explained elsewhere regarding Noach, that the fact that "there are those who interpret him for disgrace" is not a matter of "disgrace"—for although Noach himself, because he was in a very low and inferior generation, did not pray for the people of his generation like Avraham and Moshe (since "he did not have anyone to rely upon for merit like Moshe"), and thus there was no deficiency in him; nevertheless, so that a stumbling block would not come from this, Heaven forbid, in the generations that followed him—to say that it is not a deficiency if one does not pray for the people of the generation—therefore, some say that one is forced to say that this matter is a matter of "disgrace." This very fact, that they interpret such conduct as disgrace, is the "merit" of Noach, that Noach himself wants them to emphasize that one must pray for the people of the generation, in order to ensure that no deficiency comes from this in another generation.

And similarly in our case, the rectification of Shelomis the daughter of Dibri is in the very fact that the Torah publicized her (and with such emphasis), for from this comes the instruction and the ultimate caution for the women of Israel regarding the magnitude of modesty even in speech, etc., and this itself is the merit and praise of Shelomis.]

C. But seemingly, the aforementioned teaching of our Sages, that the verse here informs us of "the praise of the Jewish people, that there was not among them except for this one, and the Torah publicized and detailed it," its content is not that the praise is the lesson (the informing of the law) regarding the matter of modesty, but rather its meaning is that the verse informs and tells of the praise of the Jewish people (that they were not suspected of illicit relations) in the very fact that it publicizes the disgrace of Shelomis.

And this matter (telling of the praise of the Jewish people through the publicizing of the disgrace of Shelomis) requires understanding: how is it possible that the praise of the Jewish people should be revealed through the telling and publicizing of the disgrace of one of the children of Israel? For just as it is in the law that for the benefit and utility of the many, one does not nullify the existence of the individual, similarly, seemingly, regarding the telling of merits and disgraces, it is forced to say that the praise of the Jewish people should be revealed specifically through the disgrace of one of the Jews. Especially since one of the matters that was innovated at the Giving of the Torah among the Jewish people is learned from that which is stated: "I am Hashem your G-d"—in the singular, that everyone as an individual in their own right has importance and merit, and is not nullified and dragged after the collective, as our Sages said: "Every single person is obligated to say, 'For my sake was the world created.'"

[This is one of the differences between the other creations and the children of Israel: that in all creations, the purpose is not in themselves, and they were created only for the sake of the Jewish people and for the sake of the Torah, and just as it is in the entirety of their existence that they are not for their own sake, similarly it is in their details, that it is possible that every detail is secondary to the collective. Whereas with the Jewish people, just as the entirety of the Jewish people is not a "means" for another matter, but

## 64

the ultimate intent is in them, so it is regarding each one of them individually, that one cannot say that one Jewish person serves only as a "means" for his fellow Jew, nor for the collective and the public, but the ultimate intent is also in each and every one individually.]

And it must be that since through the fact that the Torah publicized her, the praise of the Jewish people is revealed, this is not considered a disgrace for her; on the contrary, this itself is her praise, that through her the Torah announces the praise of the Jewish people, that they were not suspected of illicit relations: "A locked garden is my sister, my bride, a locked spring, a sealed fountain."

An example of this is the prohibition of *bal tashchis* (wanton destruction): the prohibition is only when the act is by way of destruction and ruin, but when through the ruin a rectification comes, there is no prohibition of *bal tashchis* here. And similar to the law in the work of Shabbos that "all who destroy are exempt" ["If one tore garments or burned them or broke vessels by way of destruction, he is exempt"], but "one who tears in his anger, etc., is liable, because he settles his mind through this matter and his impulse is calmed, and since his anger is calmed by this matter, he is like one who is rectifying, and he is liable."

D. Furthermore, it can be said, by way of introduction to the fact that we find a dispute whether a person is obligated to place himself in a state of possible danger for the sake of saving his fellow from certain danger. Some say that the dispute does not concern the collective of Israel, for then it is certain that the individual is obligated to place himself in danger for the sake of saving the collective of Israel. It can be said that this is not because the saving of the collective of Israel overrides the life of the individual, but because since every individual is a part of the collective, a part of the public, it turns out that the saving of the collective of Israel is also the saving of the individual.

And if these things are said regarding danger to life, all the more so in the matter of telling and publicizing a matter of disgrace. And furthermore, there is not only salvation here, but the telling of the "praise of the Jewish people" which touches upon the foundation of the collective of Israel, that the beginning of the selection and creation of the Jewish people was in the manner of "a locked garden is my sister, my bride, a locked spring, a sealed fountain," that they were not suspected of illicit relations. This is not disgrace for the sake of praise alone, but the praise (of the collective of Israel) is the praise (also) of Shelomis herself.

E. And in the inner dimension of the matters, it can be said:
It is known that all things in the world are included in three levels: 1) Things of holiness, 2) Permitted things, 3) Forbidden things. The difference between them is: the meaning of "permitted" is "that which is not bound and held by the *chitzonim*," meaning that a person can refine the thing from *kelipas nogah* and elevate the good within it to holiness, similar to "one who eats the fat meat of an ox and drinks spiced wine to expand his mind for Hashem and His Torah, etc., or in order to fulfill the mitzvah of the delight of Shabbos and Yom Tov, then the vitality of the meat and the wine, which was influenced by *kelipas nogah*, is refined and ascends to Hashem like a burnt offering and a sacrifice," as is explained in *Tanya*.

Whereas "forbidden" is that which is forbidden and bound by the *kelipos*, "forbidden foods, etc., which are from the three completely impure *kelipos*, they are forbidden and bound by the *chitzonim* forever, and they do not ascend from there until their day comes and death will be swallowed up forever."

However, even in this time, it is possible that they will ascend to holiness, as the Alter Rebbe writes there, through "performing such a great *teshuvah* that intentional sins become for him like actual merits, which is *teshuvah* from love from the depths of the heart, etc., since through this he comes to this great love," therefore even the intentional sins ascend to become merits.

And similarly, it is understood in our case regarding Shelomis, that since "no one cast away will be cast away from Him," the rectification of that which was the opposite of modesty and holiness is when this act brings about modesty and the ultimate modesty—the announcement of the "praise of the Jewish people," that "they were not suspected of illicit relations," and regarding all of them it is said, "A locked garden is my sister, my bride, a locked spring, a sealed fountain."

Similar to the aforementioned in the words of the Alter Rebbe, that through *teshuvah* from love, intentional sins become merits, "since through this he comes to this great love."


## בהר

## 72

### Behar

A. The Rambam writes at the end of *Hilchos Avadim* (and the conclusion of *Sefer Kinyan*): "It is permitted to work a Canaanite slave with rigor. Nevertheless, it is a *midas chassidus* and the way of wisdom that a person should be merciful and pursue justice, not make his yoke heavy upon his slave, and not distress him. He should feed him and give him to drink from all food and drink. The early Sages would give the slave from every dish that they themselves ate, and would prioritize the food of their animals and slaves over their own meal, as it is written : 'As the eyes of slaves to the hand of their masters, as the eyes of a maidservant to the hand of her mistress.' Furthermore, he should not degrade him with his hand or with words, etc., and should not shout at him or be angry, but rather speak to him gently and listen to his grievances, etc." (At the conclusion of the *halacha*, the Rambam speaks at length about the quality of mercy inherent in the seed of Avraham Avinu, as will be discussed below in section 3).

Now, regarding the Rambam’s statement that "it is a *midas chassidus* and the way of wisdom that a person should be merciful... and not make his yoke heavy upon his slave"—implying that even the "way of wisdom" and intellect necessitate this conduct (as indicated by his precise phrasing later in the passage, "the early Sages," rather than "the early *chassidim*")—this can be explained simply, based on the Rambam’s words in the previous *halacha* regarding the law that "a master may say to his Canaanite slave, 'Work for me, but I will not feed you.'" The Rambam states: "Slaves that his wife brought into the marriage as *nichsei melog* (usufruct property), he is obligated to provide for, for that was the condition upon which he accepted them; for if he does not feed them, they will die and run away, and he is not liable for their loss." From this, it is understood how sound intellect necessitates a merciful approach toward the slave, because if one acts with cruelty, there is a concern that the slave will fall ill or run away, and the like.

However, based on this, the opposite requires explanation: Since even according to the "way of wisdom" one should not act with cruelty toward a Canaanite slave, why did the Rambam attribute this matter to *midas chassidus*—implying that this conduct depends on the master's quality of kindness and mercy? For even one who is not merciful, but is merely wise and shrewd, would act this way due to the "way of wisdom," so as not to lose his property, as mentioned above.

[Even though one could say that the Rambam’s intent in using the term *midas chassidus* is that this is conduct *lifnim mishuras hadin* (beyond the letter of the law)—since by law it is permitted to work him with rigor—nevertheless, and especially from the lengthiness later in the *halacha* regarding the quality of mercy in the seed of Avraham Avinu, that "they are merciful to all" (as discussed in section 3), it is understood that the Rambam’s intent in using the term *midas chassidus* here is also for conduct characterized by abundant kindness and mercy.]

## 73

This implies that the foundation of this conduct is specifically in *midas chassidus*. That is to say, even though this conduct is mandated by the "way of wisdom," this is only true for one who conducts himself with *midas chassidus* (for whom such conduct is mandated not only by his quality of mercy, but also by his wisdom). But for one who is not merciful, the calculation of the "way of wisdom" alone is insufficient to lead to this conduct.

B. This will be understood by first noting another precision in the Rambam’s words here. Following the conduct of the early Sages, who "would give the slave from every dish that they themselves ate," he writes, "and they would prioritize the food of the animals and the slaves over their own meal."

Seemingly, what do "animals" have to do with this here?

Furthermore, it is difficult to understand: the simple phrasing of the Rambam implies that the act of "prioritizing, etc." is part of the conduct of the early Sages. It has already been noted that in the Gemara, this is not brought as the conduct of the "early Sages," but rather as a law : "It is forbidden for a person to eat before giving food to his animal, as it is written : 'And I will give grass in your field for your cattle, and then you shall eat and be satisfied.'" Moreover: (a) The Gemara only mentions "his animal," whereas the Rambam adds "slaves". (b) The Gemara cites the verse "And I will give grass, etc.," while the Rambam cites "As the eyes of slaves, etc." [It is understood that the two changes are interdependent—in the verse "And I will give grass, etc.," only "your cattle" is mentioned (not slaves), whereas here the Rambam is primarily concerned with "slaves," and therefore he brought the verse "As the eyes of slaves, etc."].

Based on this, it is even more difficult: why did the Rambam bring the conduct of the "early Sages" regarding animals here in relation to "slaves" ?

It can be said that by combining animals with slaves, the Rambam intends to emphasize the reason why conduct characterized by kindness and mercy toward a Canaanite slave is in the category of *midas chassidus* (and not merely conduct according to the "way of wisdom").

For according to intellect, there is no reason for a person to prioritize the food of his animal over his own meal. On the contrary, according to the "way of wisdom," animals "were not created except to serve me", and they are

## 74

subordinate to man; one should eat first, and only afterward comes the animal's food. Rather, this conduct is connected to the person's abundant kindness and mercy (as will be discussed in section 4).

The same applies to the general conduct toward a Canaanite slave: the fact that the Torah permitted working a Canaanite slave with rigor—"work that has no limit and work that he does not need" (even though, seemingly, it is cruelty)—is because the slave's work includes performing "degrading tasks that are specifically for slaves to do" [which is why it is forbidden to work a Hebrew slave in these tasks, since we were commanded, "You shall not work him with the work of a slave"]. In order that there be no lack in the matter of slavery (that the slave should not prevent himself from doing degrading tasks), there is room to conduct oneself with him in such a way that he feels his status as a slave. Therefore, the Torah permitted working him with rigor (even though it causes pain to the slave), because it relates to the slavery itself.

From this, it is understood that from the perspective of the "way of wisdom" alone, there is room to say that one *must* specifically perform work with rigor. Only one who is graced with *midas chassidus* and is "merciful and pursues justice" conducts himself with great mercy even toward his slave, to the point that he conducts himself with him in a manner of equality [similar to the obligation regarding a Hebrew slave "to make them equal to himself in food and drink, etc."], and even more—that he prioritizes his slave's food over his own meal (even though such conduct is, on the face of it, the opposite of the very concept of slavery). Such conduct is only possible due to the master's abundant mercy.

C. According to this explanation, one must also explain the lengthiness of the Rambam’s language at the end of this *halacha*, where he states:

"Cruelty and harshness are not found except

## 75

among the idol-worshipping nations. But the seed of Avraham Avinu, which is Yisrael, upon whom the Holy One, Blessed be He, bestowed the goodness of the Torah and commanded them with righteous statutes and judgments, are merciful to all. And likewise, regarding the attributes of the Holy One, Blessed be He, which we are commanded to emulate, it is written : 'And His mercies are upon all His works.' And whoever is merciful, mercy is shown to him, as it is written : 'And He will give you mercy, and have mercy on you, and multiply you.'"

Now, the Rambam brings this matter regarding the quality of mercy of the seed of Avraham (and its opposite among the nations) in several places in *Sefer HaYad*:

In *Hilchos Teshuvah* : "It is forbidden for a person to be cruel, etc. He should forgive with a whole heart, etc. This is the way of the seed of Yisrael and their upright heart. But the idolaters, who are uncircumcised in heart, are not so; rather, 'they keep their wrath forever,' etc."

In *Hilchos Issurei Biah* : "Anyone who possesses brazenness or cruelty, hates the creatures, and does not perform kindness for them, we are highly suspicious of him that perhaps he is a Gibeonite. For the signs of Yisrael, the holy nation, are that they are bashful, merciful, and performers of kindness."

In *Hilchos Matnos Aniyim* : "For charity is a sign of the righteous, the seed of Avraham Avinu, as it is written : 'For I have known him, so that he may command his children to perform charity, etc.' And anyone who is cruel and does not show mercy, one should be suspicious of his lineage, for cruelty is not found except among the nations, etc."

One must examine the reason why, specifically here in *Hilchos Avadim*, he adds two details:

(a) "And they are Yisrael, upon whom the Holy One, Blessed be He, bestowed the goodness of the Torah and commanded them with righteous statutes and judgments." This implies that it is not enough that the seed of Avraham Avinu are merciful and performers of kindness by nature, nor that Avraham Avinu commanded his children and his household after him to perform charity, etc.—rather, there must also be the fact "that the Holy One, Blessed be He, bestowed upon them the goodness of the Torah and commanded them with righteous statutes and judgments." Seemingly, on the contrary, in this *halacha* the Rambam is speaking about conduct *lifnim mishuras hadin*, beyond what is mandated by the statutes and judgments of the Torah (since according to the law of the Torah, it is permitted to work a Canaanite slave with rigor). If so, seemingly the foundation of this conduct is (not in the judgments of the Torah, but) in the quality of kindness and mercy inherent in the souls of the people of Yisrael.

(b) "And likewise, regarding the attributes of the Holy One, Blessed be He, which we are commanded to emulate, it is written: 'And His mercies are upon all His works.'" In other places, he does not write that the quality of mercy is among the attributes of the Holy One, Blessed be He, which we are commanded to emulate (as he writes in *Hilchos Deos* : "Just as He is called merciful, so too you be merciful").

## 76

D. According to what we have explained, the explanation for this is as follows:

The relationship of respect, etc., toward a Canaanite slave is not only not mandated by the "way of wisdom," but even the natural quality of mercy planted in the soul of the seed of Avraham is insufficient for this. For even these mercies are limited mercies according to the nature and definition of a created being, and these mercies do not apply to a Canaanite slave in matters that are the opposite of the slavery itself.

It can be said that the Rambam hinted at this by bringing the fact that the early Sages would prioritize the food of the animals over their own meal. For even though it is possible that a merciful and kind person might be stirred by feelings of mercy for a living creature and not cause it pain, nevertheless, it is understood and simple that his mercy toward them is not greater than his love for himself and his household. If so, it is understood that the fact that the early Sages would prioritize the food of their animals over their own meal is not because of natural feelings of mercy, but rather this is a different type of mercy.

This is what the Rambam adds here: "(And they are Yisrael) upon whom the Holy One, Blessed be He, bestowed the goodness of the Torah and commanded them with righteous statutes and judgments, are merciful to all," and he adds, "And likewise, regarding the attributes of the Holy One, Blessed be He, which we are commanded to emulate, it is written: 'And His mercies are upon all His works.'" By this, he means that even though this conduct is not mandated by human mercy and kindness (even of the soul of a Yisrael, the seed of Avraham), nevertheless, "the Holy One, Blessed be He, bestowed upon them the goodness of the Torah." The "goodness of the Torah" is not only goodness mandated by human intellect and the nature of people, but as mandated by the wisdom of the Holy One, Blessed be He. Furthermore, we were commanded to emulate the attributes of the Holy One, Blessed be He. Because of this, there is no limit to the mercy of Yisrael; "they are merciful to all," to the point that they prioritize the food of their animals over their own meal.

E. Explaining the matter:

Regarding the mercy of the Holy One, Blessed be He—there are two aspects: (a) They are not mercy in the simple sense, a feeling of mercy, but rather conduct of mercy toward the created beings, as the Rambam explained at length in *Moreh Nevuchim*, that the attributes of the Holy One, Blessed be He, "are descriptions of His actions," that "He performs actions similar to actions performed by us as a result of attributes, that is, from psychological traits, not that He, may He be exalted, possesses psychological traits." (b) On the other hand—since we are speaking of His attributes, may He be blessed, just as He, may He be blessed, has no end, so too "His holy attributes are not in a state of limitation and finality, but in a state of *Ein Sof*", and there is no limit to His mercies; "His mercies do not cease".

This is the very reason that "His mercies are upon all His works." For with the attribute of flesh and blood, where the cause of mercy is the situation of the other (upon whom he has mercy), there is a great difference in the quality of mercy according to the value of the situation of the one receiving mercy, to the point that something very far from the stature of man might not have its situation stir a feeling of mercy in a person. Not so with Him, may He be blessed, the Creator of all; nothing occupies a place (by itself), and His mercies, may He be blessed, are due to the attributes of the Holy One, Blessed be He. Therefore, there is no limit to His mercies, and they are upon all His works.

This is also the innovation in the "goodness of the Torah" that the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded us in, that it possesses these two qualities: (a) The command is for conduct in a manner of mercy, that even in a situation where the human heart is not capable of being stirred by a feeling of mercy in this matter, nevertheless, one must conduct himself according to the "righteous statutes and judgments" of the Torah. (b) And in these mercies—there is no measure or limit.

This is also the reason the Rambam adds, "And likewise, regarding the attributes of the Holy One, Blessed be He, which we are commanded to emulate, it is written: 'And His mercies are upon all His works,'" to emphasize that this is the content of the "goodness of the Torah" that the Holy One, Blessed be He, bestowed upon us—not only to conduct ourselves with mercy and kindness mandated by human nature (even the nature of a Yisrael), but to emulate His attributes, may He be blessed, "to the extent of one's ability", above human limitation.

From this perspective, a person prioritizes the food of his animal even over his own meal, and likewise, his mercy toward his slave is in a manner of extreme respect (the opposite of the concept of slavery), mercy beyond measurement.

And through such mercy, "whoever is merciful, mercy is shown to him, as it is written: 'And He will give you mercy, and have mercy on you, and multiply you'"—the mercy of the Holy One, Blessed be He, and specifically abundant mercy.

F. It is known from our Sages regarding the verse : "He declares His word to Yaakov, His statutes and His judgments to Yisrael," that "what He does, He tells Yisrael to do and observe." From this, it is understood also in our case, that the Holy One, Blessed be He, as it were, also fulfills this *halacha* in the Rambam regarding the conduct of a master with his Canaanite slave.

The matter is as follows:

It is explained in several places that three types of slaves—a Canaanite slave, a Hebrew slave, and a Hebrew maidservant—allude to three levels and modes in the service of the children of Yisrael, who are "servants of Hashem". The Canaanite slave, regarding whom our Sages said, "A slave prefers licentiousness," is one who has no feeling in his heart for arousal in the service of Hashem, and his service is only by way of coercion and acceptance of the yoke.

Regarding this slave, the letter of the law dictates that it is permitted to work him with rigor in order to break the slave's materiality (from which comes his desire for licentiousness, etc.). This is what the Rambam informs us: that from the perspective of the "higher *midas chassidus* and way of wisdom," not only does one not work him with rigor, Heaven forbid, but on the contrary, one conducts himself with him out of mercy and justice, to feed him and give him to drink "from all food and from all drink... from every dish." It is understood that this includes not only necessary things, but also matters of *taanug* (just as the master regarding himself, who is not satisfied with necessities but has delicacies for the sake of *taanug*).

Furthermore—that he even prioritizes the food of his slave over his own meal. That is to say, even before the Holy One, Blessed be He, derives *taanug* from the service of Yisrael, which is called "My offering, My bread for My fire" (which in a broader sense includes all matters of service of Hashem, which cause "pleasure before Me, that I spoke and My will was done")—He gives to Yisrael His people, even one whose service is at the level of a "Canaanite slave," all his needs, and in a manner of *taanug*.

(From a *sicha* of Shabbos Parshas Yisro, 5745)


## בחוקותי

## 79

### Bechukosai

1. Regarding the verse, "And the land shall yield its produce," our Sages taught in Toras Kohanim: "Not as it functions now, but as it functioned in the days of Adam HaRishon. And how do we know that the land is destined to be sown and yield fruit on the very same day? The verse states, 'He has made a remembrance for His wonders.' And so it says, 'Let the earth bring forth grass, herb yielding seed'—this teaches that on the day it was sown, on that very day it yielded fruit. 'And the tree of the field shall yield its fruit'—not as it functions now, but as it functioned in the days of Adam HaRishon... on the day it is planted, on that very day it yields fruit. How do we know that the tree itself is destined to be eaten? The verse states, 'a fruit tree.' If this is to teach that it yields fruit, has it not already been stated, 'yielding fruit'? If so, why is it stated 'a fruit tree'? Rather, just as the fruit is eaten, so too the tree is eaten. And how do we know that even barren trees are destined to yield fruit? The verse states, 'And the tree of the field shall yield its fruit.'"

This theme—the miraculous wonders and superior states that will exist in the physical world in the future—is found in the words of our Sages in many places. For example, in Maseches Kesuvos: "Eretz Yisrael is destined to bring forth cakes and woolen garments... Wheat is destined to grow as tall as a palm tree and rise to the tops of the mountains... Wheat is destined to be like the two kidneys of a large ox," and similar descriptions.

One must understand: In describing the state of the future, the Rambam writes, "The entire world will be occupied only with knowing Hashem. Therefore, Yisrael will be great sages and know the hidden things, and they will attain knowledge of their Creator to the extent of human capacity, as it is said, 'For the earth shall be full of the knowledge of Hashem as the waters cover the sea.'" It is understood that when Bnei Yisrael are in such a state, the aforementioned physical miracles and wonders regarding the fruits of the land (which the Rambam describes by saying, "all delicacies will be as common as dust") hold no significance. Even though it is understood that there is a benefit to these things—for when "goodness is bestowed in abundance," it serves as a means "so that (Yisrael) may be free for Torah and its wisdom, and have no oppressor or distractor"—still, why did Chazal dwell so extensively on the superiority of the physical delicacies that will exist in the future, as if they were something uniquely and supremely important in their own right?

2. The commentators raised a similar question regarding the reward for mitzvos explained in our parsha: "If you walk in My statutes and keep My mitzvos and perform them—then I will give your rains in their season..." The text elaborates on the details of the reward for the mitzvos, most of which are physical things. Yet, the essence of the perfection of reward is spiritual benefit and goodness, which is the true good and benefit.

Some commentators explain this according to the view of the Rambam, that "all these promises (in our parsha) are not the essence of the reward. Rather, all the goodness mentioned here in this chapter speaks only of the removal of obstacles. That is to say, if you keep My mitzvos, I will remove from you all obstacles—such as wars, illnesses, hunger, and grief—so that you will be able to serve Hashem without any hindrance. But the essence of the reward of the World to Come is not mentioned here, so that one will serve his Creator for His sake, and not because of that reward or out of fear of punishment."

However, according to many other commentators, this parsha does indeed speak of the reward for fulfilling Torah and mitzvos.

It can be said that the reason the reward specified in the text consists primarily of physical promises is understood by first clarifying the question: Why did the text elaborate on the description of the reward for Torah study and the fulfillment of mitzvos, especially since the ultimate goal of avodah is to serve "for its own sake"—"Do not be like servants who serve the master in order to receive a reward, but be like servants who serve the master not in order to receive a reward"?

The answer is that this avodah is a very great level, and as the Rambam writes, "This level is a very great level, and not every sage merits it." But the beginning of avodah is avodah that is not "for its own sake," as our Sages said: "A person should always engage in Torah and mitzvos even if not for their own sake." "Therefore, when teaching children, women, and the general masses of the people, one teaches them only to serve out of fear and in order to receive a reward, until their knowledge increases..." Since the Torah speaks in the language of the majority, and the Torah study and mitzvah fulfillment of the majority of Bnei Yisrael is not "not in order to receive a reward," the reward for engaging in Torah and mitzvos is explained in the Torah at length.

Just as this is true regarding reward in general, it is also true regarding the content of the reward: Since most Bnei Yisrael at this stage generally do not have a yearning for spiritual goals and rewards, the Torah explains primarily the physical reward and physical promises, in which everyone has an understanding and a taste, and this will spur them on to Torah study and the fulfillment of mitzvos.

In this way, we can also explain the future: that our Sages marvel at the time of the future with physical miracles and wonders that will exist then, because

## 81

even in the future, not everyone will immediately reach a lofty level of spiritual reward, and certainly not the level of avodah "for its own sake"—avodah out of love. Rather, even then, the order of ascents will be "from strength to strength," and a person will not be born in a state of ultimate perfection in the knowledge and attainment of Hashem and avodah out of love. Therefore, there is a need for physical miracles and wonders to precede and spur them on to the fulfillment of Torah and mitzvos.

3. But on the face of it, this explanation is somewhat forced. For the parsha of Bechukosai, as well as all the parshios in the Torah where the promise of reward for mitzvos is explained, were said to all of Yisrael, including those who are at a lofty level in their avodah of Hashem. According to the above, it would turn out that this parsha is irrelevant to those who have an attainment of spiritual reward, and certainly irrelevant to those whose avodah is "not in order to receive a reward," and only relevant to one whose attainment is only in physical goods, and only that will spur him to fulfill Torah and mitzvos.

Even though "the Torah speaks in the language of the majority," it is more reasonable to explain it in a way that is relevant to everyone. Furthermore, there is surely some lesson in this even for the minority.

Moreover: In explaining the verse, "If you walk in My statutes and keep My mitzvos and perform them," our Sages explained (as brought in Rashi) that this means "to be toiling in Torah." And not only that, but the toil in Torah is for the purpose of keeping and fulfilling. Thus, the essence of this verse refers to Torah study and the fulfillment of mitzvos not in the ordinary way, but at a lofty level of Torah study and mitzvah fulfillment—study through toil, and keeping and fulfilling through toil.

Especially according to what is explained in the seforim regarding the interpretation of "Bechukosai" (by way of remez) as being from the word *chakikah* (engraving): that the Torah study and mitzvah fulfillment here is not only in the manner of toil and exertion, but in the manner of engraving, where the Torah and mitzvos are engraved in his heart and in his entire essence. This is like the superiority of engraved letters over written letters: not only are engraved letters more unified with the stone in which they are engraved than written letters are with the parchment upon which they are written, but there is no reality here other than the stone in which the letters are engraved. In other words, the Torah study hinted at here is not only in a way where the person and the Torah are like two things joined together, but furthermore, that the person has no reality, and his entire reality is the Torah he is studying. It is understood that for such a person, whose Torah study is in the manner of engraving, the mitzvos he fulfills are also in such a manner, penetrating his entire reality.

From all this, it is understood that we are speaking of a most lofty level of Torah study and mitzvah fulfillment. If so, it is simple that for him, nothing takes up space other than Torah and mitzvos, and certainly not physical rewards and promises. How is it possible that to a person at such a level of avodah of Hashem, one says that because "you walk in My statutes and keep My mitzvos and perform them,"

## 82

he will receive the reward of "I will give your rains in their season," and all the physical promises regarding bodily matters, etc.?

4. The explanation for this can be said to be as follows:
Regarding the Torah, it is said, "It is your life and the length of your days." The meaning is not only that the Torah brings life to a person in this world and the World to Come, but that it is the very life of every single Jew. This means that for a Jew, the Torah is not something added to his reality or one of the other details of his life, but is his essence and his life itself.

Man is divided into several parts, and in general—head, body, and foot. The head is the place where his lofty powers reside, the intellect, etc., as well as the senses of sight and hearing, etc. The body is a vessel and dwelling for the emotions within it, and so on. In the feet, there is only the power of walking, which is the lowest power, in which the superiority of man is not evident. However, this is only in relation to the specific powers of man. But the essence of his soul's vitality, which is above the specific powers, is found in the entire body equally, from his head to his foot.

The reason for this is that the soul's vitality is the essence of man, and therefore it is found in every single point of the body equally.
[Similar to the story in the Gemara regarding the *taanug* (pleasure) in the soul, which acts also upon and in the heel of the foot: "A good report makes the bones fat." Because *taanug* is of the essence of the soul, it is therefore found and acts throughout the entire person, even down to his heel.]

Furthermore: The proof and test of whether a certain thing originates from the vitality that is the essence of man is that it comes to expression not only in his lofty powers, but also in his lesser powers, down to the heel of the foot.
— An example of this: A person who contemplates something that brings him joy—the proof that the joy has penetrated his entire essence and he is truly happy is when the joy is expressed not only in his thoughts and speech, but also in his actions, down to dancing with his feet. And similarly with other emotions, such as love and fear, or, to distinguish, sorrow, and the like. —

From this, we can also understand regarding the fact that the Torah is our life—that the revelation and expression of the fact that the Torah is the vitality and the entire essence of a person is when the Torah acts and influences not only his soul's matters—spiritual and lofty matters—but also in bodily matters and in all his physical affairs that pertain to him.

If the reward for Torah study "for its own sake" were only that he would merit spiritual levels and lofty matters, there would be no complete expression that the Torah is his entire reality, because the reward would be like a natural result of his Torah engagement, for which reason

## 83

it is a spiritual reward in exchange for his spiritual avodah. But when physical goods are bestowed upon him because of his spiritual avodah in Torah and mitzvos—"I will give your rains in their season and the tree of the field, etc."—then it is seen and recognized that Torah and mitzvos are not just study and action added to his reality, or at most just a detail in his reality, but the Torah is his entire essence and vitality. Therefore, engaging in it brings goodness and benefit in all his affairs, even in the physical matters of this world.

The reason why the Torah is the vitality and essence of man is because "the Torah and the Holy One, blessed be He, are all one." Therefore, just as the Holy One, blessed be He, is the true reality, from the truth of Whose existence all existences were brought into being—from Whom all the superiorities and types of perfection in the entire creation came—so too is it regarding the Torah, which brings good and benefit to all existences, all types of benefit, both spiritual and physical.

According to this, it turns out that, on the contrary, it is precisely in the matter of physical reward that it is emphasized that the Torah is in the manner of "Bechukosai"—from the word *chakikah*, that the Torah is engraved in him, until it becomes his very reality.

5. Based on the above, the connection of the physical promises in the verses and the words of our Sages regarding the future is also explained, such as the aforementioned statements: "On the day it is planted, on that very day it yields fruit," "Eretz Yisrael is destined to bring forth cakes and woolen garments," and the like—for this precisely indicates the perfection of the state of Bnei Yisrael in their Torah study and mitzvah fulfillment.

The fact that in the present time, a person's affairs come about in a manner of toil and exertion, and a long time passes until they yield fruit, both in herbs and in the fruits of the tree, is not only because of the sin of the Tree of Knowledge, which caused the state of the world to descend—"Cursed is the ground for your sake; in sorrow shall you eat of it... in the sweat of your face shall you eat bread"—but because the physical matters of the world are not "vessels," so to speak, for the influence of the Holy One, blessed be He. This means that even a person who studies Torah and fulfills mitzvos as he should, and is bestowed with all good even in his physical needs, it does not come immediately, because his physical matters are not unified with their root in spirituality, the influence of Hashem.

This is the innovation that will be in the future in the days of Moshiach: that there will be no gap between the action and the growth in the physical, because in the future, physical things will be in unity with their root and source—the word of Hashem.

This distinction between avodah in the manner of engraving and unity in the present time, and the innovation that will be in the future, is understood by way of an analogy from the difference between engraving on a stone and engraving that goes from one side to the other. As the difference is explained, that engraved letters, for example in a gemstone, even though the letters are of the essence of the stone, nevertheless, since these letters have a place of attachment in the stone, they have a resemblance and connection to written letters—which also conceal somewhat, they

## 84

darken the "purity" of the gemstone—whereas letters engraved from one side to the other (like the letters on the Tablets) have no place of attachment and are completely separated from written letters.

And so it is in the analogy: When something is engraved in a person like the letters of engraving in a gemstone, meaning that it is engraved and penetrates within him (not like written letters which are on the parchment) but does not penetrate into his very essence, then just as it is not his very essence, so too it is with the result of this, which is not in a way that penetrates to all his external matters. But when Torah and mitzvos are engraved in a person from one side to the other, which is his entire essence, this penetrates and acts from one side to the other, in all his powers and affairs, down to all the things in the world. Therefore, it is seen and revealed even in the physical world that there is no gap between the person's action and the fruits that grow, for the influence of the Holy One, blessed be He, is bestowed immediately and directly without any gap at all.

This matter will be revealed in the future, when the engraving that was on the Tablets will be revealed, as it is said, "Engraved (*charut*) on the Tablets," and our Sages said, "Do not read *charut* (engraved) but *cheirut* (freedom)—freedom from the Angel of Death, etc." This freedom will be in the future, in the true and complete Redemption through Moshiach Tzidkeinu, speedily in our days, truly.

(From the sichos of the month of Shevat, 5739)


## ברכת כ"ק אדמו"ר שליט"א לאנ"ש שיחיו שברכו אותו, אור ליום ועש"ק פ' צו, י"א ניסן ה'תש"נ

## 89

### The Rebbe’s Blessing to Anash who blessed him, Eve of Shabbos Parshas Tzav, 11 Nissan, 5750

**— After Maariv —**

1. First and foremost — *Amen, ken yehi ratzon* to everything said above, and especially to the blessing for the *Geulah Amitis Ve’Hashleimah* through *Moshiach Tzidkeinu*, for which one specifically says the expression: “*Amen, ken yehi ratzon*” (as is understood from the words of the Rambam). This includes these blessings in the letters and the like.

May the *Ribono shel Olam* fulfill the desires of their hearts for good, and the desires of the hearts of every man and woman of Israel, within the collective of Israel — men, women, and children. This includes, and especially, the blessings and good wishes that were spoken of here just now, up to and especially the blessing for the *Geulah Amitis Ve’Hashleimah*.

And surely the *Ribono shel Olam* will fulfill His promise, “And I will bless those who bless you”, that whoever blesses is blessed with the blessing of the *Hakadosh Baruch Hu*, whose “addition is greater than the essence”, and which comes from His full, open, holy, and expansive hand — for every man and woman of Israel, in everything they need. This certainly includes health of the body and health of the *neshamah* for each and every one with all their household, with expansion in physical *parnassah* and in spiritual *parnassah*, including matters of the season — that all preparations for *Chag HaPesach* should be with joy and goodness of heart, both physically and spiritually.

2. This is further strengthened when the desires of the heart come from a multitude of Jews — and were spoken aloud, openly and in public, in the presence of many tens of groups of Israel, and “in the multitude of people is the King’s glory,” the King of Kings, the *Hakadosh Baruch Hu*. As is known, the virtue of a *tzibbur* of Israel, as it is written, “Seek Hashem when He is found, call upon Him when He is near,” and our Sages of blessed memory taught that in a *tzibbur*, the *Hakadosh Baruch Hu* is “found” and “near” throughout the entire year.

And especially since, in addition to this virtue in the *nefesh* (the request and blessing of the *tzibbur*), there is added the virtue of the place and the time: the place — this *beis haknesses*, *beis hamedrash*, and *beis maasim tovim* of *Kvod Kedushas Moreinu V’Rabbeinu Nasi Doreinu*; and the time — in the month of *Nissan*, which is connected with “

## 90

miracles of miracles” (as is understood from the words of the Gemara near the end and conclusion of *Maseches Brachos*), and within this itself — in the year 5750, the acronym of which is *Tehei Shnas Nissim* (it shall be a year of miracles).

And by virtue of all these qualities (in *nefesh*, place, and time) — all these blessings and good wishes will surely be accepted and fulfilled in practice with greater intensity and strength.

This includes, and especially, the primary blessing (as was spoken of earlier, aloud and in public): the *Geulah Amitis Ve’Hashleimah* through *Moshiach Tzidkeinu*, which is also a matter of the season: “In *Nissan* they were redeemed, and in *Nissan* they are destined to be redeemed.” Especially since in the month of *Nissan* itself, two *Shabbosos* have already passed, and we are preparing and standing in immediate proximity to *Chag HaPesach* together with “our redemption and the ransom of our souls” — the true freedom of every Jew and of all Jews, in the *Geulah Amitis Ve’Hashleimah* through *Moshiach Tzidkeinu*.

And immediately, right now — as the *nitisas koach* (empowerment) of the *Parsha* of the week — “*Tzav*,” “there is no *Tzav* but an expression of urging, immediately and for generations.” From this, one comes directly, and especially through the reading immediately (before this *Mincha* prayer), to “And it came to pass on the eighth day” — the concept of the *Geulah*.

And simply put — that “with our youth and with our elders, etc., with our sons and with our daughters”, “and they came with the clouds of heaven”, all Jews will go to the *Eretz HaKodesh*, and to Jerusalem the Holy City, and to the Holy Mountain, and to the *Beis Hamikdash*, and to the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, “and there we will perform before You the sacrifices of our obligations, the *temidim* according to their order and the *musafim* according to their law, etc., as is the commandment of Your will.”

And even before this — immediately as Jews bless other Jews as mentioned above — all blessings are fulfilled in practice, similar to the ruling in the *Shulchan Aruch* that when Jews make a good resolution, the *Ribono shel Olam* immediately gives the reward (as if it had been performed in practice and in its entirety). And one goes “from strength to strength,” taking along all the aforementioned blessings — “And I will bless those who bless you, etc.,” whoever blesses is blessed, as mentioned above.

3. To all these blessings, there is added even more due to the fact that in the month of *Nissan* itself, we stand on the eleventh day of the month of *Nissan*, the *Nasi* for the tribe of *Asher*, who “shall provide royal delicacies” — the perfection in the drawing down of blessings. To add, *Asher* is connected specifically with *Chag HaPesach*, as hinted at by the fact that *Asher* has the *gematria* of

## 91

*Taka* (501) (as explained in the writings of the *AriZal*) — which *Taka* indicates a drawing down (blessing) that is above the *Seder Hishtalshelus*, and this is drawn down below and acts within the world (as this was on *Chag HaPesach*).

And within this itself, there is added even more on 11 *Nissan* of this year, when we begin to recite Chapter 89 in *Tehillim*, where it states, “The world shall be built with *chesed*,” which is connected with *Avraham Avinu* (“*Echad* was *Avraham*”), to whom the *Ribono shel Olam* promised, “And I will bless those who bless you”.

And also the beginning of the psalm — “*Maskil l’Eisan HaEzrachi*” — is connected with *Avraham*, and connected with the drawing down of blessing to every Jew, as is known the *Torah* of the *Alter Rebbe* on the verse (“*Maskil l’Eisan HaEzrachi*”), transmitted through *Kvod Kedushas Moreinu V’Rabbeinu*.

Up to the conclusion of the psalm — “Blessed be Hashem forever, *Amen* and *Amen*,” that all these blessings are drawn down from (the Name) *Havayah* into the world, and with a double confirmation — “*Amen* and *Amen*”.

And simply put — that the *Ribono shel Olam* blesses all Jews with all blessings from *Aleph* to *Tav*, and adds to them with joy and goodness of heart in all matters of good. Starting with everyone having true *nachas* from themselves, and from all their household, and from their entire surroundings, and having the most essential *nachas* — that which our Father in Heaven has from all His children, all the sons and daughters of Israel, each and every one of whom belongs to the “people I have formed for Myself, they shall recount My praise” (as discussed above at length), “they shall recount My praise” already now, and continuing this “they shall recount My praise” in the *Eretz HaKodesh* in the *Geulah Amitis Ve’Hashleimah*.

May it be His will that immediately, right now, everyone should see openly how within their own four cubits all the blessings of the *Ribono shel Olam* are fulfilled, and in their entirety,

## 92

from His full, open, holy, and expansive hand, in good that is visible and revealed, and immediately after that — “they shall go from strength to strength”, until “he shall appear before *Elokim* in Zion,” and with joy and goodness of heart.

And in the language of the conclusion of the psalm (89) — “*Amen* and *Amen*.”

[Kvod Kedushas Admur Shlita distributed to each person: A *Kuntres* for 11 Nissan of this year (special edition), together with a dollar bill, to give it (or its equivalent) to *tzedakah*.]


## בס"ד. ברכת כ"ק אדמו"ר שליט"א לאנ"ש שיחיו שברכו אותו

## 93

### With the help of Heaven. The blessing of the Rebbe to Anash who blessed him

**After Maariv**

1. Just as in Torah the order is that "with whatever one concludes... one opens," so too must it be with Yidden: that "with whatever one concludes" becomes the continuation and the beginning and the opening of the matter that follows, in a manner of ongoing activity (until—for all the years to come).

Since we have just concluded with "And I will bless them," the "opening" is also with this concept:

"And I will bless them": Hashem says in the Torah (and the verse does not depart from its literal meaning) that He Himself will bless all those who bless [the Jewish people], and with the blessing of the Ribono shel Olam—from His full, open, holy, and wide hand, without any measurement or limitation of place or time. Consequently, it arrives immediately and truly, and together with this, "And I will bless them"—as this is above measurement and limitation (from "I," Hashem Himself)—it is drawn down (the word *bracha* comes from *hemshech*, drawing down) to Yidden below, and in the manner of "I will bless them" (in general, without specifics), which is—all blessings, to every Yid and to all Yidden, whether in general matters or in specific matters, down to the smallest details.

This means that "And I will bless them" includes two matters, two extremes: from "I" (Hashem Himself) which is above all measurement and limitation, comes "I will bless them," a blessing and drawing down to Yidden below.

For in every blessing and drawing down, two things are required: the blessing must come from a place that is higher than the limitations of the recipient (which is why he needs to arrive at the blessing), and together with this, it must be drawn down into the recipient in his inwardness, according to his nature and his needs.

2. The blessing of Hashem ("And I will bless them")—and in both matters: (a) a blessing that is above measurement and limitation, (b) which is drawn down to every Yid below—is specifically emphasized in the prayer and blessing of Moshe at the beginning of the 90th Psalm in Tehillim—the first of the "eleven psalms" that Moshe said—which begins: "A prayer of Moshe, the man of Elokim," and concludes (with the prayer of Moshe) "And may the pleasantness of Hashem our G-d be upon us, and the work of our hands establish for us, and the work of our hands, establish it."

Being a prayer (*tefillah l'Moshe*)—and also a blessing—from "Moshe, the man of Elokim," it is understood that this contains within it the power of (a blessing of) "Elokim," similar to (kivayachol) "And I (Hashem Himself) will bless them."

And as is emphasized in the content of the prayer and the blessing, and in its conclusion—"And may the pleasantness of Hashem our G-d be upon us"—that "upon us"—every Yid and all Yidden—should be the revelation of the "pleasantness of Hashem our G-d." For "pleasantness" (*noam*) (*noam ila'ah*) is the level of Atik, and the inwardness of Atik, until—the Essence of Hashem ("I," "I will bless them"), and this should be drawn down into "me'on" (the letters of *noam*), Malchus, until into the "work of our hands." The power for this comes from "A prayer of Moshe, the man of Elokim," the prayer of the wealthy one, which reaches into the Essence of Hashem,

## 95

and therefore it is understood that this is a blessing above all measurement and limitation of place and time, since it comes from "Moshe, the man of Elokim" and in the manner of "the pleasantness of Hashem our G-d be upon us."

And as is understood from this, with this blessing Moshe blessed the Yidden that there should be the indwelling of the Shechinah in the Mishkan—"May it be Your will that the Shechinah dwell in the work of your hands, and may the pleasantness of Hashem our G-d be upon us, etc.", and in the manner of "establish it upon us, etc.," fortified in a foundational manner—with permanence, forever and for all eternity (as is understood from the explanation in Tanya regarding "the Shechinah dwells," that this is with permanence and eternity), and "And I will dwell (with permanence) in their midst," within each and every one of Yisroel.

[And it may be said that the power for this comes from the fact that the blessing is from "Moshe, the man of Elokim," for specifically from Hashem comes the power to accomplish the indwelling of the Shechinah in a manner of permanence and eternity.]

According to this, it emerges that in "A prayer of Moshe, etc." are emphasized the two aforementioned matters (in "And I will bless them") which are required in a blessing: together with the fact that "A prayer of Moshe, the man of Elokim" is a blessing above all measurement and limitation—it is a blessing which is drawn down from Moshe to every Yid, and a general blessing—the blessing that "the Shechinah should dwell in the work of your hands."

## 100

And through this—one also has the power for the avodah to accomplish in the world (which was created with the name Elokim), and to reveal how the world is a vessel for Elokus, by making all matters of the world a vessel to carry out His mission in the world through the fulfillment of Torah and mitzvos, the revelation of the Shechinah "in the work of your hands" in a manner of permanence and eternity, until it becomes a dwelling place for Him (for His Essence) in the lower realms, in the lowest realm, below which there is no lower.

And the power for this comes, as stated above, from "A prayer of Moshe, the man of Elokim," from Moshe Rabbeinu, and from the Moshe within every Yid in Yisroel, who has within himself both matters of "Moshe" and "the man of Elokim," and he is blessing and drawing down powers (in a manner of absorption into the inwardness, as above) into the avodah of the "work of our hands" in both matters—in the revelation of the light of the neshamah, the light of holiness, and in the avodah with the vessel of the body and the refinement of the matters of the world, that both should be "establish it," fortified in a foundational manner—the revelation of the "Foundation of foundations and Pillar of wisdoms" (the initials of the Tetragrammaton) (which is above the name Elokim) in the reality of the world, that one knows and sees how "all that exists from the heavens and the earth and what is between them only exists from the truth of His existence," "And the Tetragrammaton is the true G-d," "He alone is the truth, etc." [similar to "And the truth of the Tetragrammaton is forever"], "This existence is the G-d of the world, the Master of all the earth," until in a manner that "the earth is full of the knowledge of the Tetragrammaton as the waters cover the sea."

8. The aforementioned matter of "A prayer of Moshe" stands with even more emphasis and revelation in this generation of ours—when we have the power of the prayers and blessings and avodah of our Rebbes, the Nesi'im, the extension of Moshe in every generation, until the Rebbe, the Nasi of our generation, the extension of Moshe in our generation,

And especially when we stand after forty years from his passing (in the year 5710), when "Hashem has given you a heart to know and eyes to see and ears to hear," a person stands on the intent of his Rebbe,

who gives the power to the level of Moshe in every Yid in our generation—and especially through the revelation of the inwardness of the Torah—to reveal how he is "Moshe, the man of Elokim," and how "And may the pleasantness of Hashem our G-d be upon us, and the work of our hands establish for us, and the work of our hands, establish it," the revelation of the level of *noam* (the inwardness of Atik), through the study of Torah, and especially the inwardness of the Torah,

## 101

and the avodah of spreading Torah and Yiddishkeit and spreading the wellsprings outward, in a manner that this accomplishes "establish it" in the "work of our hands," in all levels therein,

starting from the avodah with oneself—through the fact that every Yid reveals (by the power of the Nasi of our generation) how he is a messenger of Hashem, and a messenger of a person (the Higher One) is like him ("Moshe, the man of Elokim"), which comes about through the fact that a Yid—as a messenger of Hashem—utilizes his powers for the avodah of Hashem, such that the powers are only like a vessel, a "messenger," to carry out the mission and will of Hashem, in the study of Torah, the revealed Torah and the inwardness of the Torah [including and especially the halachos in the Torah (the level of the Tetragrammaton in the Torah), such as the Sefer of the Rambam and the like], and the fulfillment of mitzvos with *hiddur*,

and thus he also accomplishes in the world, that all matters of the world become a vessel to carry out the mission of Hashem;

and through the fact that the messenger does his avodah in completeness in conjunction with his ten powers—this becomes Mashiach (in gematria, *shaliach* plus ten), until the revelation of Mashiach in actuality, when there will be the completeness of the revelation "And may the pleasantness of Hashem our G-d be upon us, etc."

9. In all of this, there is even more emphasis in this year, 5751, the initials of which are "it shall be a year of wonders I will show him":

The root of miraculous conduct comes from the name of the Tetragrammaton, which is above natural conduct that comes from the name Elokim (in gematria, *hateva*—nature).

The regular order in the world is natural conduct. However, there are times when Hashem performs miracles, from the expression "I will raise my banner," "like the banner on the mountains," which raises up and is

## 103

the details, and raises up Yidden (and the "work of our hands") above their status and situation even when they were in a year of miracles (when even then they were raised up), and in a manner of "(wonders) I will show him," that one sees it openly, he points with his finger and says "this."

And to add: the order of the letters of the year number is (57)51, first "wonders" and then "I will show him." But Yidden have (also) added and changed the order, that one should say "I will show him wonders" (as the verse says). And it may be said the difference: in "wonders I will show him," the main emphasis is on the wonders, and the wonders—"I will show him," one sees them in a manner of sight.

In "I will show him wonders," the main emphasis is on the "I will show him," how Hashem Himself shows in a manner of sight—the truth and inwardness of every thing, and while this is in a manner of "I will show him" (through Hashem Himself), one sees (automatically) how this is all—"wonders" (whether natural conduct, whether miraculous conduct, whether the events of the past, or whether the wonders that will be in the future).

Until it becomes "I will show him"—the revelation and sight in Hashem Himself, kivayachol—"And your eyes shall see your Teacher," sight with physical eyes, until this also permeates the natural conduct and physicality of a Yid. And when there is this "I will show him" in ("And may the pleasantness of Hashem our G-d be upon us," until in) Hashem Himself, one sees how everything (whether nature, whether miracle) is "wonders" ("establish it") of Hashem.

10. In the year of "I will show him wonders" itself, this is even more emphasized in the month of Nissan, which points to "miracles of miracles," miracles even in comparison to miracles (similar to the wonders of the future, as above),

and in the month of Nissan itself—after Shabbos HaGadol, "in which a great miracle occurred" (greatness in the miracle itself), and already after the tenth of Nissan (when the "great miracle" happened in actuality, on the Shabbos before Pesach, the tenth of Nissan),

and we are already at the eve of the "eleventh day" of the month of Nissan, the "Nasi for the children of Asher," whose matter is—"And he shall give royal delicacies," every Yid is blessed—even in the last moments of galus—with "royal delicacies," the matter of pleasure ("And may the pleasantness"), and "royal delicacies" specifically—the completeness of pleasure, whether in physicality or in spirituality, including—"royal delicacies" in the avodah of Hashem, the drawing down of pleasure also into the order and completeness of the avodah of a king, the initials of which are *moach, lev, kaved* (brain, heart, liver), the brain rules over the heart, and then it is drawn down into the liver (the source of the blood, "the blood is the soul"), such that there is a completeness of the form and state of the person—so also in this there is an additional blessing, of the drawing down of pleasure ("royal delicacies").

And it is revealed how every Yid is a prince (until a "king" himself)—a child of the King of kings, Hashem, and as it is written, "And all your children shall be students of the Tetragrammaton," which applies to every Yid (who is included in "your children")—and as the matter of "all your children, etc." is explained in the wedding discourses of the Rebbe Rayatz; and being a "prince," he receives "royal delicacies" in completeness, the completeness of pleasure.

And it is appropriate to add and connect this with the well-known saying of the Baal Shem Tov, that every Yid is as precious to Hashem as an only child born to elderly parents in their old age—for seemingly, the analogy of "elderly parents in their old age" is not understood regarding Hashem, and in the language of Chazal: "Is there old age before Him?!"

And it may be said, according to the known fact that "elder" (*zaken*) is connected with (wisdom, until with) Atik (Yomin), for Atik is a term for elder (as it is written, "And the Ancient of Days sat, etc."), the inwardness of Keter, until with the inwardness of Atik, the Essence of pleasure (the level of eleven, one and not in the count); and it may be said that the aforementioned analogy (that a Yid is an only child born to elderly parents, etc.) hints at how a Yid is connected with the level of *zaken* (Atik) above, which is one thing with the Essence and Being—Yisroel and Hashem are entirely one. And therefore there is also drawn down to him (the prince) "royal delicacies"—the Essence of pleasure (Atik).

And especially through the fact that Yidden have added in the study of Torah in general, including and especially—the study of the inwardness of the Torah, through which the drawing down of the revelation of the "Essence of Atik" into Yidden occurs.

And certainly one will continue to add in the study of Torah and especially the inwardness of the Torah—and also in the fulfillment of mitzvos with *hiddur*—in a manner that is above measurement and limitation, and in this itself—one adds more and more, "they go from strength to strength," that regardless of the conduct until now in a manner of "miracle," and of "I will raise my banner," raised up relative to his regular miraculous conduct from before, he ascends higher and even higher in a manner of "wonders" in the Torah ("Open my eyes and I will behold wonders from Your Torah"), until "wonders I will show him," and even more—"I will show him wonders," that there is the study of Torah in a manner of sight, until sight in Elokus, in the Giver of the Torah ("And your eyes shall see your Teacher"), and this brings the revelation of "wonders" in the Torah.

And through this one receives measure for measure and many times over from Hashem, that "I will show him wonders" literally—Hashem Himself shows him, in a manner of pointing with His finger and saying "this": here you have the miracles and wonders that I, Hashem, have given you, just as "elderly parents" give to the "only child born in their old age," from the level of Atika Kadisha,

until Hashem Himself stands in revelation, and with what is He "occupied," kivayachol (or without a kivayachol)—with "I will show him," showing every Yid "wonders," that that which has happened to him until now was also "wonders," and certainly the wonders that come after (after the "I will show him"),

until—the wonders in the true and complete Geulah, and in a manner of "I will show him," and after—even higher "wonders" come, etc., until Ein Sof.

11. In all of this, there is an addition through the fact that we will now conclude (as usual) by making each of those present here (and through them—all of Yisroel) a messenger of a mitzvah in the mitzvah of tzedakah (which is equal to all the mitzvos), and as discussed above that through

the fact that every Yid as a messenger of Hashem, will utilize his ten powers in completeness in fulfilling his mission—he brings (messenger plus ten in gematria) Mashiach, and in addition to "gematria" (which is in concealment), in actual revelation, in sensory sight,

and immediately and truly Mashiach takes all Yidden out of galus, "with our youth and with our elders, etc., with our sons and with our daughters," in the true and complete Geulah, and we go to Eretz HaKodesh, to Yerushalayim the holy city, to the holy mountain, to the Third Beis HaMikdash, "the Sanctuary, Hashem, which Your hands have established,"

where one has in ultimate completeness all the blessings of "A prayer of Moshe, the man of Elokim... and the work of our hands establish for us, and the work of our hands, establish it"—as the blessing of Moshe (and Aharon) regarding the indwelling of the Shechinah in the Mishkan, "And they blessed the people," "And may the pleasantness of Hashem our G-d be upon us, may it be Your will that the Shechinah dwell in the work of your hands"—that the completeness of "the Shechinah should dwell in the work of your hands" will be in the Third Beis HaMikdash, "And when it is built—Moshe and Aharon will be with us,"

and it becomes (in example (but in ascent and addition relative to) how it was at the time of the indwelling of the Shechinah in the Mishkan) "And all the people saw and they sang and they fell on their faces"—a revelation of Elokus in a manner of sight, until this brings out in them a song, and such a revelation that is higher than inwardness (until "and they fell on their faces").

May it be the will, that even in our final deeds and avodah in the last moments of galus there should be the indwelling of the Shechinah "in the work of your hands," including in the mission of tzedakah that we will now give,
